<p>In context of <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a>, a <b>satellite</b> is an artificial object which has been intentionally placed into <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>. Such objects are sometimes called <b>artificial satellites</b> to distinguish them from <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a> such as Earth's <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.</p>
<p>In 1957 the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> launched the world's first artificial satellite, <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>. Since then, about 6,600 satellites from more than 40 countries have been launched. According to a 2013 estimate, 3,600 remained in orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-Rising-11-13_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-Rising-11-13-1">[1]</a></sup> Of those, about 1,000 were operational;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-2">[2]</a></sup> while the rest have lived out their useful lives and became <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>. Approximately 500 operational satellites are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-Earth_orbit" title="Low-Earth orbit">low-Earth orbit</a>, 50 are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medium-Earth_orbit" title="Medium-Earth orbit">medium-Earth orbit</a> (at 20,000 km), and the rest are in <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a> (at 36,000 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FCain_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FCain-3">[3]</a></sup> A few large satellites have been launched in parts and assembled in orbit. Over a dozen <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a> have been placed into orbit around other bodies and become artificial satellites to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, a few <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.</p>
<p>Satellites are used for many purposes. Common types include military and civilian <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">Earth observation satellites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navigation_satellite" title="Navigation satellite">navigation satellites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather satellites</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Space_telescope" title="Space telescope">space telescopes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">Space stations</a> and human <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> in orbit are also satellites. Satellite orbits vary greatly, depending on the purpose of the satellite, and are classified in a number of ways. Well-known (overlapping) classes include low Earth orbit, <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a>.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> that throws a satellite into orbit. Usually it lifts off from a <a href="/wiki/Launch_pad" title="Launch pad">launch pad</a> on land. Some are launched at sea from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submarine-based_launch_vehicle" title="Submarine-based launch vehicle">submarine</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Sea_Launch" title="Sea Launch">mobile maritime platform</a>, or aboard a plane (see <a href="/wiki/Air_launch_to_orbit" title="Air launch to orbit">air launch to orbit</a>).</p>
<p>Satellites are usually semi-independent computer-controlled systems. Satellite subsystems attend many tasks, such as power generation, <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_thermal_control" title="Spacecraft thermal control">thermal control</a>, telemetry, <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> and orbit control.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_conceptions">Early conceptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early conceptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_cannonball" title="Newton's cannonball">Newton's cannonball</a>", presented as a "thought experiment" in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Treatise_of_the_System_of_the_World" title="A Treatise of the System of the World">A Treatise of the System of the World</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> was the first published mathematical study of the possibility of an artificial satellite.</p>
<p>The first fictional depiction of a satellite being launched into orbit was a <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short story</a> by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Everett_Hale" title="Edward Everett Hale">Edward Everett Hale</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brick_Moon" title="The Brick Moon">The Brick Moon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The idea surfaced again in <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Begum%27s_Fortune" title="The Begum's Fortune">The Begum's Fortune</a></i> (1879).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1986_CPA_5712.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="618" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/1986_CPA_5712.jpg/220px-1986_CPA_5712.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/1986_CPA_5712.jpg/330px-1986_CPA_5712.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/1986_CPA_5712.jpg/440px-1986_CPA_5712.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1986_CPA_5712.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1903, <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky" title="Konstantin Tsiolkovsky">Konstantin Tsiolkovsky</a> (1857–1935) published <i>Exploring Space Using Jet Propulsion Devices</i> (in <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <i>Исследование мировых пространств реактивными приборами</i>), which is the first academic treatise on the use of rocketry to launch spacecraft. He calculated the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> required for a minimal orbit, and that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-stage_rocket" title="Multi-stage rocket">multi-stage rocket</a> fuelled by liquid <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellants</a> could achieve this.</p>
<p>In 1928, <a href="/wiki/Herman_Poto%C4%8Dnik" title="Herman Potočnik">Herman Potočnik</a> (1892–1929) published his sole book, <i>The Problem of Space Travel — The Rocket Motor</i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i>Das Problem der Befahrung des Weltraums — der Raketen-Motor</i>). He described the use of orbiting spacecraft for observation of the ground and described how the special conditions of space could be useful for scientific experiments.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConstellationGPS.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="192" data-file-width="240" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/ConstellationGPS.gif" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConstellationGPS.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation depicting the orbits of GPS satellites in <a href="/wiki/Medium_Earth_orbit" title="Medium Earth orbit">medium Earth orbit</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a 1945 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_World" title="Wireless World">Wireless World</a></i> article, the English science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a> (1917–2008) described in detail the possible use of <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellites</a> for mass communications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> He suggested that three geostationary satellites would provide coverage over the entire planet.</p>
<p>The US military studied the idea of what was referred to as the <i>earth satellite vehicle</i> when Secretary of Defense <a href="/wiki/James_Forrestal" title="James Forrestal">James Forrestal</a> made a public announcement on 29 December 1948, that his office was coordinating that project between the various services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artificial_satellites">Artificial satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Artificial satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sputnik_asm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="1094" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Sputnik_asm.jpg/240px-Sputnik_asm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Sputnik_asm.jpg/360px-Sputnik_asm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Sputnik_asm.jpg/480px-Sputnik_asm.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sputnik_asm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>: The first artificial satellite to orbit Earth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first artificial satellite was <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, launched by the Soviet Union on 4 October 1957, and initiating the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik_program" title="Sputnik program">Sputnik program</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a> as chief designer. This in turn triggered the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> between the Soviet Union and the United States.</p>
<p>Sputnik 1 helped to identify the density of high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere#Temperature_and_layers" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmospheric layers</a> through measurement of its orbital change and provided data on radio-signal distribution in the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>. The unanticipated announcement of <i>Sputnik 1'</i>s success precipitated the <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_crisis" title="Sputnik crisis">Sputnik crisis</a> in the United States and ignited the so-called Space Race within the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sputnik_2" title="Sputnik 2">Sputnik 2</a> was launched on 3 November 1957 and carried the first living passenger into orbit, a dog named <a href="/wiki/Laika" title="Laika">Laika</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_animals_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_animals-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May, 1946, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_RAND" title="Project RAND">Project RAND</a> had released the <a href="/wiki/Preliminary_Design_of_an_Experimental_World-Circling_Spaceship" title="Preliminary Design of an Experimental World-Circling Spaceship">Preliminary Design of an Experimental World-Circling Spaceship</a>, which stated, "A satellite vehicle with appropriate instrumentation can be expected to be one of the most potent scientific tools of the Twentieth Century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The United States had been considering launching orbital satellites since 1945 under the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Aeronautics" title="Bureau of Aeronautics">Bureau of Aeronautics</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>'s Project RAND eventually released the report, but considered the satellite to be a tool for science, politics, and propaganda, rather than a potential military weapon. In 1954, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Erwin_Wilson" title="Charles Erwin Wilson">Secretary of Defense</a> stated, "I know of no American satellite program."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In February 1954 Project RAND released "Scientific Uses for a Satellite Vehicle," written by R.R. Carhart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This expanded on potential scientific uses for satellite vehicles and was followed in June 1955 with "The Scientific Use of an Artificial Satellite," by H.K. Kallmann and W.W. Kellogg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the context of activities planned for the <a href="/wiki/International_Geophysical_Year" title="International Geophysical Year">International Geophysical Year</a> (1957–58), the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> announced on 29 July 1955 that the U.S. intended to launch satellites by the spring of 1958. This became known as <a href="/wiki/Project_Vanguard" title="Project Vanguard">Project Vanguard</a>. On 31 July, the Soviets announced that they intended to launch a satellite by the fall of 1957.</p>
<p>Following pressure by the <a href="/wiki/American_Rocket_Society" title="American Rocket Society">American Rocket Society</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>, and the International Geophysical Year, military interest picked up and in early 1955 the Army and Navy were working on <a href="/wiki/Project_Orbiter" title="Project Orbiter">Project Orbiter</a>, two competing programs: the army's which involved using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter-C_IRBM" title="Jupiter-C IRBM">Jupiter C rocket</a>, and the civilian/Navy Vanguard Rocket, to launch a satellite. At first, they failed: initial preference was given to the Vanguard program, whose first attempt at orbiting a satellite resulted in the explosion of the launch vehicle on national television. But finally, three months after Sputnik 2, the project succeeded; <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">Explorer 1</a> became the United States' first artificial satellite on 31 January 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1961, three-and-a-half years after the launch of Sputnik 1, the Air Force used resources of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Space_Surveillance_Network" title="United States Space Surveillance Network">United States Space Surveillance Network</a> to catalog 115 Earth-orbiting satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early satellites were constructed as "<a href="/wiki/One-off" title="One-off">one-off</a>" designs. With growth in <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous</a> (GEO) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_satellite" title="Communication satellite">satellite communication</a>, multiple satellites began to be built on <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_satellite_buses" title="Comparison of satellite buses">single model platforms</a> called <a href="/wiki/Satellite_bus" title="Satellite bus">satellite buses</a>. The first standardized satellite bus design was the <a href="/wiki/HS-333" title="HS-333">HS-333</a> GEO commsat, launched in 1972.</p>
<p>The largest artificial satellite currently orbiting the Earth is the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg/220px-ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg/330px-ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg/440px-ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ESTCube-1_illustration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1U <a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">CubeSat</a> <a href="/wiki/ESTCube-1" title="ESTCube-1">ESTCube-1</a>, developed mainly by the students from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tartu" title="University of Tartu">University of Tartu</a>, carries out a tether deployment experiment in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Surveillance_Network">Space Surveillance Network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Space Surveillance Network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Space_Surveillance_Network" title="United States Space Surveillance Network">United States Space Surveillance Network</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Space_Surveillance_Network" title="United States Space Surveillance Network">United States Space Surveillance Network</a> (SSN), a division of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Strategic_Command" title="United States Strategic Command">United States Strategic Command</a>, has been tracking objects in Earth's orbit since 1957 when the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> opened the <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">Space Age</a> with the launch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik_I" title="Sputnik I">Sputnik I</a>. Since then, the SSN has tracked more than 26,000 objects. The SSN currently tracks more than 8,000 man-made orbiting objects. The rest have re-entered Earth's atmosphere and disintegrated, or survived re-entry and impacted the Earth. The SSN tracks objects that are 10 centimeters in diameter or larger; those now orbiting Earth range from satellites weighing several tons to pieces of spent rocket bodies weighing only 10 pounds. About seven percent are operational satellites (i.e. ~560 satellites), the rest are <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The United States Strategic Command is primarily interested in the active satellites, but also tracks space debris which upon reentry might otherwise be mistaken for incoming missiles.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-military_satellite_services">Non-military satellite services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Non-military satellite services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are three basic categories of non-military satellite services:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant.26Meadows_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant.26Meadows-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fixed_satellite_services">Fixed satellite services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fixed satellite services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_Service_Satellite" title="Fixed Service Satellite">Fixed satellite services</a> handle hundreds of billions of voice, data, and video transmission tasks across all countries and continents between certain points on the Earth's surface.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobile_satellite_systems">Mobile satellite systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mobile satellite systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mobile satellite systems help connect remote regions, vehicles, ships, people and aircraft to other parts of the world and/or other mobile or stationary communications units, in addition to serving as navigation systems.</p>
<h3><span id="Scientific_research_satellites_(commercial_and_noncommercial)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_research_satellites_.28commercial_and_noncommercial.29"><span id="Research_satellites"></span> Scientific research satellites (commercial and noncommercial)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Scientific research satellites (commercial and noncommercial)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scientific research satellites provide meteorological information, land survey data (e.g. remote sensing), Amateur (HAM) Radio, and other different scientific research applications such as earth science, marine science, and atmospheric research.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_satellite" title="Astronomical satellite">Astronomical satellites</a></b> are satellites used for observation of distant planets, galaxies, and other outer space objects.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Biosatellite" title="Biosatellite">Biosatellites</a></b> are satellites designed to carry living organisms, generally for scientific experimentation.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">Communications satellites</a></b> are satellites stationed in space for the purpose of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>. Modern communications satellites typically use <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molniya_orbit" title="Molniya orbit">Molniya orbits</a> or <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">Low Earth orbits</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">Earth observation satellites</a></b> are satellites intended for non-military uses such as <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environmental</a> monitoring, <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Map_making" title="Map making">map making</a> etc. (See especially <a href="/wiki/Earth_Observing_System" title="Earth Observing System">Earth Observing System</a>.)</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Navigation_Satellite_System" title="Global Navigation Satellite System">Navigational satellites</a></b> are satellites which use radio time signals transmitted to enable mobile receivers on the ground to determine their exact location. The relatively clear line of sight between the satellites and receivers on the ground, combined with ever-improving electronics, allows satellite navigation systems to measure location to accuracies on the order of a few meters in real time.</li>
<li><b>"Killer Satellites"</b> are satellites that are designed to destroy enemy warheads, satellites, and other space assets.</li>
<li><b>Crewed <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> (spaceships)</b> are large satellites able to put <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> into (and beyond) an orbit, and return them to Earth. Spacecraft including <a href="/wiki/Spaceplane" title="Spaceplane">spaceplanes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Reusable_launch_system" title="Reusable launch system">reusable systems</a> have major <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">landing</a> facilities. They can be used as transport to and from the orbital stations.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miniaturized_satellites" title="Miniaturized satellites">Miniaturized satellites</a></b> are satellites of unusually low masses and small sizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> New classifications are used to categorize these satellites: minisatellite (500–100 kg), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsatellite_(spaceflight)" title="Microsatellite (spaceflight)">microsatellite</a> (below 100 kg), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanosatellite" title="Nanosatellite">nanosatellite</a> (below 10 kg).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance_satellite" title="Reconnaissance satellite">Reconnaissance satellites</a></b> are <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation_satellite" title="Earth observation satellite">Earth observation satellite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellite</a> deployed for <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> or <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">intelligence</a> applications. Very little is known about the full power of these satellites, as governments who operate them usually keep information pertaining to their reconnaissance satellites classified.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recovery_satellite" title="Recovery satellite">Recovery satellites</a></b> are satellites that provide a recovery of reconnaissance, biological, space-production and other payloads from orbit to Earth.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2116" data-file-width="3319" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/220px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/330px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg/440px-International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station_after_undocking_of_STS-132.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
International Space Station</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">Space stations</a></b> are artificial orbital structures that are designed for <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human beings</a> to live on in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>. A space station is distinguished from other crewed spacecraft by its lack of major propulsion or landing facilities. Space stations are designed for medium-term living in orbit, for periods of weeks, months, or even years.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tether_satellite" title="Tether satellite">Tether satellites</a></b> are satellites which are connected to another satellite by a thin cable called a <a href="/wiki/Tether" title="Tether">tether</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">Weather satellites</a></b> are primarily used to monitor Earth's weather and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_types">Orbit types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Orbit types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_orbits" title="List of orbits">List of orbits</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4300" data-file-width="4300" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg/350px-Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg/525px-Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg/700px-Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orbits_around_earth_scale_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Various earth orbits to scale; cyan represents low earth orbit, yellow represents medium earth orbit, the black dashed line represents geosynchronous orbit, the green dash-dot line the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS) satellites, and the red dotted line the orbit of the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> (ISS).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first satellite, <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, was put into orbit around Earth and was therefore in <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">geocentric orbit</a>. By far this is the most common type of orbit with approximately 1,459<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCS-SatDB_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-UCS-SatDB-20">[20]</a></sup> artificial satellites orbiting the Earth. Geocentric orbits may be further classified by their altitude, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a>.</p>
<p>The commonly used altitude classifications of geocentric orbit are <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">Low Earth orbit</a> (LEO), <a href="/wiki/Medium_Earth_orbit" title="Medium Earth orbit">Medium Earth orbit</a> (MEO) and <a href="/wiki/High_Earth_orbit" title="High Earth orbit">High Earth orbit</a> (HEO). Low Earth orbit is any orbit below 2,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Km" title="Km">km</a>. Medium Earth orbit is any orbit between 2,000 and 35,786 km. High Earth orbit is any orbit higher than 35,786 km.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centric_classifications">Centric classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Centric classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Geocentric_orbit" title="Geocentric orbit">Geocentric orbit</a></b>: An orbit around the planet Earth, such as the Moon or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_satellite" title="Artificial satellite">artificial satellites</a>. Currently there are approximately 1,459<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UCS-SatDB_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-UCS-SatDB-20">[20]</a></sup> artificial satellites orbiting the Earth.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_orbit" title="Heliocentric orbit">Heliocentric orbit</a></b>: An orbit around the Sun. In our <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, all planets, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> are in such orbits, as are many artificial satellites and pieces of <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">space debris</a>. <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite#Natural_satellites_of_the_Solar_System" title="Natural satellite">Moons</a> by contrast are not in a heliocentric orbit but rather orbit their parent planet.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Areocentric_orbit" title="Areocentric orbit">Areocentric orbit</a></b>: An orbit around the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, such as by <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Mars" title="Moons of Mars">moons</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_satellite" title="Artificial satellite">artificial satellites</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>The general structure of a satellite is that it is connected to the earth stations that are present on the ground and connected through terrestrial links.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Altitude_classifications">Altitude classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Altitude classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">Low Earth orbit</a> (LEO)</b>: Geocentric orbits ranging in altitude from 180 km - 2,000 km (1,200 mi)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Medium_Earth_orbit" title="Medium Earth orbit">Medium Earth orbit</a> (MEO)</b>: Geocentric orbits ranging in altitude from 2,000 km (1,200 mi) - 35,786 km (22,236 mi). Also known as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermediate_circular_orbit" title="Intermediate circular orbit">intermediate circular orbit</a>.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_Orbit" title="Geosynchronous Orbit">Geosynchronous Orbit</a> (GEO)</b>: Geocentric circular orbit with an altitude of 35,786 kilometres (22,236 mi). The period of the orbit equals one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal day</a>, coinciding with the rotation period of the Earth. The speed is approximately 3,000 metres per second (9,800 ft/s).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/High_Earth_orbit" title="High Earth orbit">High Earth orbit</a> (HEO)</b>: Geocentric orbits above the altitude of <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">geosynchronous orbit</a> 35,786 km (22,236 mi).</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orbitalaltitudes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1656" data-file-width="4500" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Orbitalaltitudes.jpg/320px-Orbitalaltitudes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Orbitalaltitudes.jpg/480px-Orbitalaltitudes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Orbitalaltitudes.jpg/640px-Orbitalaltitudes.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orbitalaltitudes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Orbital Altitudes of several significant satellites of earth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclination_classifications">Inclination classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inclination classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Inclined_orbit" title="Inclined orbit">Inclined orbit</a></b>: An orbit whose inclination in reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_plane" title="Equatorial plane">equatorial plane</a> is not zero degrees.
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">Polar orbit</a></b>: An orbit that passes above or nearly above both poles of the planet on each revolution. Therefore, it has an inclination of (or very close to) 90 <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a>.</li>
<li><b>Polar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_synchronous_orbit" title="Sun synchronous orbit">sun synchronous orbit</a></b>: A nearly polar orbit that passes the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> at the same local time on every pass. Useful for <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">image</a> taking satellites because <a href="/wiki/Shadow" title="Shadow">shadows</a> will be nearly the same on every pass.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eccentricity_classifications">Eccentricity classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Eccentricity classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Circular_orbit" title="Circular orbit">Circular orbit</a></b>: An orbit that has an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of 0 and whose path traces a <a href="/wiki/Circle" title="Circle">circle</a>.
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbit</a></b>: An orbit that moves a spacecraft from one approximately circular orbit, usually the orbit of a planet, to another, using two engine <a href="/wiki/Impulse_(physics)" title="Impulse (physics)">impulses</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> of the transfer orbit is at the same distance from the Sun as the radius of one planet's orbit, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> is at the other. The two rocket burns change the spacecraft's path from one circular orbit to the transfer orbit, and later to the other circular orbit. This maneuver was named after <a href="/wiki/Walter_Hohmann" title="Walter Hohmann">Walter Hohmann</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Elliptic_orbit" title="Elliptic orbit">Elliptic orbit</a></b>: An orbit with an eccentricity greater than 0 and less than 1 whose orbit traces the path of an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>.
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_transfer_orbit" title="Geosynchronous transfer orbit">Geosynchronous transfer orbit</a></b>: An elliptic orbit where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> is at the altitude of a <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">Low Earth orbit</a> (LEO) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a> at the altitude of a geosynchronous orbit.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Geostationary_transfer_orbit" title="Geostationary transfer orbit">Geostationary transfer orbit</a></b>: An elliptic orbit where the perigee is at the altitude of a Low Earth orbit (LEO) and the apogee at the altitude of a geostationary orbit.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Molniya_orbit" title="Molniya orbit">Molniya orbit</a></b>: A highly elliptic orbit with inclination of 63.4° and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of half of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_day" title="Sidereal day">sidereal day</a> (roughly 12 hours). Such a satellite spends most of its time over two designated areas of the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> (specifically Russia and the United States).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Tundra_orbit" title="Tundra orbit">Tundra orbit</a></b>: A highly elliptic orbit with inclination of 63.4° and orbital period of one sidereal day (roughly 24 hours). Such a satellite spends most of its time over a single designated area of the planet.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Synchronous_classifications">Synchronous classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Synchronous classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">Synchronous orbit</a></b>: An orbit where the satellite has an orbital period equal to the average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_period" title="Rotational period">rotational period</a> (earth's is: 23 hours, 56 minutes, 4.091 seconds) of the body being orbited and in the same direction of rotation as that body. To a ground observer such a satellite would trace an <a href="/wiki/Analemma" title="Analemma">analemma</a> (figure 8) in the sky.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Semi-synchronous_orbit" title="Semi-synchronous orbit">Semi-synchronous orbit</a> (SSO)</b>: An orbit with an altitude of approximately 20,200 km (12,600 mi) and an orbital period equal to one-half of the average rotational period (Earth's is approximately 12 hours) of the body being orbited</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_orbit" title="Geosynchronous orbit">Geosynchronous orbit</a> (GSO)</b>: Orbits with an altitude of approximately 35,786 km (22,236 mi). Such a satellite would trace an <a href="/wiki/Analemma" title="Analemma">analemma</a> (figure 8) in the sky.
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">Geostationary orbit</a> (GEO)</b>: A geosynchronous orbit with an inclination of zero. To an observer on the ground this satellite would appear as a fixed point in the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clarke_orbit" title="Clarke orbit">Clarke orbit</a></b>: Another name for a geostationary orbit. Named after scientist and writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Supersynchronous_orbit" title="Supersynchronous orbit">Supersynchronous orbit</a></b>: A disposal / storage orbit above GSO/GEO. Satellites will drift west. Also a synonym for Disposal orbit.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Subsynchronous_orbit" title="Subsynchronous orbit">Subsynchronous orbit</a></b>: A drift orbit close to but below GSO/GEO. Satellites will drift east.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Graveyard_orbit" title="Graveyard orbit">Graveyard orbit</a></b>: An orbit a few hundred kilometers above <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geosynchronous" title="Geosynchronous">geosynchronous</a> that satellites are moved into at the end of their operation.
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disposal_orbit" title="Disposal orbit">Disposal orbit</a></b>: A synonym for graveyard orbit.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junk_orbit" title="Junk orbit">Junk orbit</a></b>: A synonym for graveyard orbit.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Areosynchronous_orbit" title="Areosynchronous orbit">Areosynchronous orbit</a></b>: A synchronous orbit around the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> with an orbital period equal in length to Mars' sidereal day, 24.6229 hours.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Areostationary_orbit" title="Areostationary orbit">Areostationary orbit</a> (ASO)</b>: A circular <a href="/wiki/Areosynchronous_orbit" title="Areosynchronous orbit">areosynchronous orbit</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_plane" title="Equatorial plane">equatorial plane</a> and about 17000 km (10557 miles) above the surface. To an observer on the ground this satellite would appear as a fixed point in the sky.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliosynchronous_orbit" title="Heliosynchronous orbit">Heliosynchronous orbit</a></b>: A heliocentric orbit about the Sun where the satellite's orbital period matches the Sun's period of rotation. These orbits occur at a radius of 24,360 <a href="/wiki/Gigametre" title="Gigametre">Gm</a> (0.1628 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>) around the Sun, a little less than half of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Orbital_radius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Orbital radius (page does not exist)">orbital radius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_classifications">Special classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Special classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sun-synchronous_orbit" title="Sun-synchronous orbit">Sun-synchronous orbit</a></b>: An orbit which combines altitude and inclination in such a way that the satellite passes over any given point of the planets' surface at the same local <a href="/wiki/Solar_time" title="Solar time">solar time</a>. Such an orbit can place a satellite in constant sunlight and is useful for <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">imaging</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spy_satellite" title="Spy satellite">spy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather satellites</a>.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_orbit" title="Moon orbit">Moon orbit</a></b>: The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_parameters" title="Orbital parameters">orbital characteristics</a> of Earth's Moon. Average altitude of 384,403 kilometers (238,857 mi), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_orbit" title="Elliptical orbit">elliptical</a>–inclined orbit.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pseudo-orbit_classifications">Pseudo-orbit classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pseudo-orbit classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_orbit" title="Horseshoe orbit">Horseshoe orbit</a></b>: An orbit that appears to a ground observer to be orbiting a certain planet but is actually in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital_satellite" title="Co-orbital satellite">co-orbit</a> with the planet. See asteroids <a href="/wiki/3753_Cruithne" title="3753 Cruithne">3753</a> (Cruithne) and <a href="/wiki/2002_AA29" title="2002 AA29">2002 AA<sub>29</sub></a>.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exo-orbit" title="Exo-orbit">Exo-orbit</a></b>: A maneuver where a spacecraft approaches the height of orbit but lacks the velocity to sustain it.
<ul>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital_spaceflight" title="Suborbital spaceflight">Suborbital spaceflight</a></b>: A synonym for exo-orbit.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_transfer_orbit" title="Lunar transfer orbit">Lunar transfer orbit</a> (LTO)</b></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">Prograde orbit</a></b>: An orbit with an inclination of less than 90°. Or rather, an orbit that is in the same direction as the rotation of the primary.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">Retrograde orbit</a></b>: An orbit with an inclination of more than 90°. Or rather, an orbit counter to the direction of rotation of the planet. Apart from those in <a href="/wiki/Sun-synchronous_orbit" title="Sun-synchronous orbit">sun-synchronous orbit</a>, few satellites are launched into retrograde orbit because the quantity of fuel required to launch them is much greater than for a prograde orbit. This is because when the rocket starts out on the ground, it already has an eastward component of velocity equal to the rotational velocity of the planet at its launch <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Halo_orbit" title="Halo orbit">Halo orbit</a></b> and <b><a href="/wiki/Lissajous_orbit" title="Lissajous orbit">Lissajous orbit</a></b>: Orbits "around" <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_subsystems">Satellite subsystems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Satellite subsystems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The satellite's functional versatility is imbedded within its technical components and its operations characteristics. Looking at the "anatomy" of a typical satellite, one discovers two modules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant.26Meadows_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grant.26Meadows-17">[17]</a></sup> Note that some novel architectural concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Fractionated_spacecraft" title="Fractionated spacecraft">Fractionated spacecraft</a> somewhat upset this taxonomy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_bus_or_service_module">Spacecraft bus or service module</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Spacecraft bus or service module">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacecraft_bus" title="Spacecraft bus">bus module</a> consists of the following subsystems:</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_subsystem">Structural subsystem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Structural subsystem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The structural subsystem provides the mechanical base structure with adequate stiffness to withstand stress and vibrations experienced during launch, maintain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structural_integrity" title="Structural integrity">structural integrity</a> and stability while on station in orbit, and shields the satellite from extreme temperature changes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro-meteorite" title="Micro-meteorite">micro-meteorite</a> damage.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Telemetry_subsystem">Telemetry subsystem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Telemetry subsystem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> subsystem (aka Command and Data Handling, C&amp;DH) monitors the on-board equipment operations, transmits equipment operation data to the earth control station, and receives the earth control station's commands to perform equipment operation adjustments.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_subsystem">Power subsystem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Power subsystem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The power subsystem consists of solar panels to convert solar energy into electrical power, regulation and distribution functions, and batteries that store power and supply the satellite when it passes into the Earth's shadow. Nuclear power sources (<a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">Radioisotope thermoelectric generator</a> have also been used in several successful satellite programs including the <a href="/wiki/Nimbus_program" title="Nimbus program">Nimbus program</a> (1964–1978).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_control_subsystem">Thermal control subsystem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Thermal control subsystem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_thermal_control" title="Spacecraft thermal control">Spacecraft thermal control</a></div>
<p>The thermal control subsystem helps protect electronic equipment from extreme temperatures due to intense sunlight or the lack of sun exposure on different sides of the satellite's body (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Optical_solar_reflector" title="Optical solar reflector">optical solar reflector</a>)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_and_orbit_control_subsystem">Attitude and orbit control subsystem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Attitude and orbit control subsystem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">Attitude control</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">Spacecraft propulsion</a></div>
<p>The attitude and orbit control subsystem consists of sensors to measure vehicle orientation; control laws embedded in the flight software; and actuators (reaction wheels, <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">thrusters</a>) to apply the <a href="/wiki/Torque" title="Torque">torques</a> and forces needed to re-orient the vehicle to a desired attitude, keep the satellite in the correct orbital position and keep antennas positioning in the right directions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communication_payload">Communication payload</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Communication payload">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The second major module is the communication payload, which is made up of transponders. A transponder is capable of :</p>
<ul>
<li>Receiving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uplink" title="Uplink">uplinked</a> radio signals from earth satellite transmission stations (antennas).</li>
<li>Amplifying received radio signals</li>
<li>Sorting the input signals and directing the output signals through input/output signal <a href="/wiki/Multiplexer" title="Multiplexer">multiplexers</a> to the proper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downlink" title="Downlink">downlink</a> antennas for retransmission to earth satellite receiving stations (antennas).</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_life">End of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: End of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When satellites reach the end of their mission (this normaly occurs within 3 or 4 years after launch), satellite operators have the option of de-orbiting the satellite, leaving the satellite in its current orbit or moving the satellite to a <a href="/wiki/Graveyard_orbit" title="Graveyard orbit">graveyard orbit</a>. Historically, due to budgetary constraints at the beginning of satellite missions, satellites were rarely designed to be de-orbited. One example of this practice is the satellite <a href="/wiki/Vanguard_1" title="Vanguard 1">Vanguard 1</a>. Launched in 1958, <a href="/wiki/Vanguard_1" title="Vanguard 1">Vanguard 1</a>, the 4th manmade satellite put in Geocentric orbit, was still in orbit as of August 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Instead of being de-orbited, most satellites are either left in their current orbit or moved to a <a href="/wiki/Graveyard_orbit" title="Graveyard orbit">graveyard orbit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> As of 2002, the FCC requires all geostationary satellites to commit to moving to a graveyard orbit at the end of their operational life prior to launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In cases of uncontrolled de-orbiting, the major variable is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flux" title="Solar flux">solar flux</a>, and the minor variables the components and form factors of the satellite itself, and the gravitational perturbations generated by the Sun and the Moon (as well as those exercised by large mountain ranges, whether above or below sea level). The nominal breakup altitude due to aerodynamic forces and temperatures is 78 km, with a range between 72 and 84 km. Solar panels, however, are destroyed before any other component at altitudes between 90 and 95 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch-capable_countries">Launch-capable countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Launch-capable countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_first_orbital_launches_by_nationality" title="Timeline of first orbital launches by nationality">Timeline of first orbital launches by nationality</a></div>
<p>This list includes countries with an independent capability to place satellites in orbit, including production of the necessary launch vehicle. Note: many more countries have the capability to design and build satellites but are unable to launch them, instead relying on foreign launch services. This list does not consider those numerous countries, but only lists those capable of launching satellites indigenously, and the date this capability was first demonstrated. The list includes the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>, a multi-national state organization, but does not include private consortiums.</p>

<td align="left"><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1600" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/23px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/35px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/46px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a></td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempted_first_launches">Attempted first launches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Attempted first launches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(May 2012)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> tried in 1957 to launch the first satellite using its own launcher before successfully completing a launch in 1958.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> tried four times in 1966–1969 to launch a satellite with its own launcher before successfully completing a launch in 1970.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> tried in 1969 to launch the first satellite using its own launcher before successfully completing a launch in 1970.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, after launching its first national satellite using a foreign launcher in 1975, tried in 1979 to launch the first satellite using its own launcher before succeeding in 1980.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> have claimed an orbital launch of a warhead in 1989, but this claim was later disproved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, after launching its first national satellite using a foreign launcher in 1985, tried to launch a satellite using its own <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=VLS_1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="VLS 1 (page does not exist)">VLS 1</a> launcher three times in 1997, 1999, and 2003, but all attempts were unsuccessful.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> claimed a launch of <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-1" title="Kwangmyŏngsŏng-1">Kwangmyŏngsŏng-1</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-2" title="Kwangmyŏngsŏng-2">Kwangmyŏngsŏng-2</a> satellites in 1998 and 2009, but U.S., Russian and other officials and weapons experts later reported that the rockets failed to send a satellite into orbit, if that was the goal. The United States, Japan and South Korea believe this was actually a <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic missile</a> test, which was a claim also made after North Korea's 1998 satellite launch, and later rejected.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (April 2013)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> The first (April 2012) launch of <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-3" title="Kwangmyŏngsŏng-3">Kwangmyŏngsŏng-3</a> was unsuccessful, a fact publicly recognized by the DPRK. However, the December 2012 launch of the "second version" of <a href="/wiki/Kwangmy%C5%8Fngs%C5%8Fng-3" title="Kwangmyŏngsŏng-3">Kwangmyŏngsŏng-3</a> was successful, putting the DPRK's first confirmed satellite into orbit.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Korea_Aerospace_Research_Institute" title="Korea Aerospace Research Institute">Korea Aerospace Research Institute</a>), after launching their first national satellite by foreign launcher in 1992, unsuccessfully tried to launch its own launcher, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korea_Space_Launch_Vehicle" title="Korea Space Launch Vehicle">KSLV (Naro)-1</a>, (created with the assistance of Russia) in 2009 and 2010 until success was achieved in 2013 by Naro-3.</li>
<li>The First European multi-national state organization <a href="/wiki/European_Launcher_Development_Organisation" title="European Launcher Development Organisation">ELDO</a> tried to make the orbital launches at <a href="/wiki/Europa_(rocket)" title="Europa (rocket)">Europa I and Europa II</a> rockets in 1968–1970 and 1971 but stopped operation after failures.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_notes">Other notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_RUS-UKR"><b><a href="#ref_RUS-UKR">^</a></b></span> <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> were parts of the Soviet Union and thus inherited their launch capability without the need to develop it indigenously. Through the Soviet Union they are also on the number one position in this list of accomplishments.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, and Ukraine launched their first satellites by own launchers from foreign <a href="/wiki/Spaceport" title="Spaceport">spaceports</a>.</li>
<li>Some countries such as <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and private companies such as <a href="/wiki/OTRAG" title="OTRAG">OTRAG</a>, have developed their own launchers, but have not had a successful launch.</li>
<li>Only twelve, countries from the list below (USSR, USA, France, Japan, China, UK, India, Russia, Ukraine, Israel, Iran and North Korea) and one regional organization (the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>, ESA) have independently launched satellites on their own indigenously developed launch vehicles.</li>
<li>Several other countries, including <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> are at various stages of development of their own small-scale launcher capabilities.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_capable_private_entities">Launch capable private entities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Satellite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Launch capable private entities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Private firm <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Sciences_Corporation" title="Orbital Sciences Corporation">Orbital Sciences Corporation</a>, with launches since 1982, continues very successful launches of its Minotaur, Pegasus, Taurus and Antares rocket programs.</li>
<li>On 28 September 2008, late comer and private aerospace firm <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> successfully launched its Falcon 1 rocket into orbit. This marked the first time that a privately built liquid-fueled booster was able to reach orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malik_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-malik-31">[31]</a></sup> The rocket carried a prism shaped 1.5 m (5 ft) long payload mass simulator that was set into orbit. The dummy satellite, known as Ratsat, will remain in orbit for between five and ten years before burning up in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malik_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-malik-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>A few other <a href="/wiki/List_of_private_spaceflight_companies" title="List of private spaceflight companies">private companies</a> are capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-orbital" title="Sub-orbital">sub-orbital</a> launches.</p>
